Pyrrole is a five-membered aromatic heterocycle with a nitrogen atom that bears a hydrogen capable of acting as a hydrogen bond donor. In many supramolecular host architectures (such as calixpyrroles, porphyrins, and related macrocycles), multiple pyrrole units are arranged so that their NH groups are oriented toward a central cavity. This arrangement allows for the formation of multiple, directional hydrogen bonds with anionic guests, such as halides, carboxylates, or phosphates. The cumulative effect of these hydrogen bonds leads to strong and selective anion binding. Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole can contribute to the stabilization of the host-guest complex through π interactions and by providing a preorganized, rigid framework that enhances binding affinity and selectivity. The effectiveness of pyrrole-based hosts in anion recognition is a central concept in supramolecular chemistry, underpinning the design of sensors, transporters, and receptors for biologically and Pyrrole is a five-membered aromatic heterocycle with a nitrogen atom that bears a hydrogen capable of acting as a hydrogen bond donor. In many supramolecular host architectures (such as calixpyrroles, porphyrins, and related macrocycles), multiple pyrrole units are arranged so that their NH groups are oriented toward a central cavity. This arrangement allows for the formation of multiple, directional hydrogen bonds with anionic guests, such as halides, carboxylates, or phosphates. The cumulative effect of these hydrogen bonds leads to strong and selective anion binding. Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole can contribute to the stabilization of the host-guest complex through π interactions and by providing a preorganized, rigid framework that enhances binding affinity and selectivity. The effectiveness of pyrrole-based hosts in anion recognition is a central concept in supramolecular chemistry, underpinning the design of sensors, transporters, and receptors for biologically and environmentally relevant anions.', 'key_concepts': ['Supramolecular chemistry', 'Pyrrole', 'Anion binding', 'Hydrogen bonding', 'Host-guest chemistry', 'Molecular recognition', 'Preorganization', 'Aromaticity'], 'tool_analysis': {'detected_terms': [], 'class_candidates': {}, 'richness_evaluations': {}}}, 'refined_classes': ['anion_binding', 'aromatic', 'aromatic_area', 'aromatic_cavity', 'aromatic_core', 'aromatic_wall', 'host-guest_chemistry', 'hydrogen_bonding', 'membrane_organization', 'molecular_recognition', 'preorganisation', 'preorganization_of_receptor_arms', 'pyrrole', 'self-organization', 'self_organization', 'supramolecular_chemistry'], 'normalized_query': NormalizedQuery(intent='explain why supramolecular hosts containing pyrrole units are effective at binding anions, focusing on the chemical and structural reasons for their anion binding efficacy', relevant_entities=['supramolecular_chemistry', 'pyrrole', 'anion_binding', 'hydrogen_bonding', 'host-guest_chemistry', 'molecular_recognition', 'preorganisation', 'aromatic'], relevant_properties=[], filters=None, query_type_suggestion='explanation'), 'query_strategy': 'tool_sequence', 'execution_plan': ToolPlan(steps=[ToolCallStep(tool='get_class_properties', params={'class_names': ['supramolecular_chemistry', 'pyrrole', 'anion_binding', 'hydrogen_bonding', 'host-guest_chemistry', 'molecular_recognition', 'preorganisation', 'aromatic']}), ToolCallStep(tool='get_related_classes', params={'class_names': ['supramolecular_chemistry', 'pyrrole', 'anion_binding', 'hydrogen_bonding', 'host-guest_chemistry', 'molecular_recognition', 'preorganisation', 'aromatic']})]), 'sparql_query': None,

Pyrrole is a five-membered aromatic heterocycle with a nitrogen atom that bears a hydrogen capable of acting as a hydrogen bond donor. In many supramolecular host architectures (such as calixpyrroles, porphyrins, and related macrocycles), multiple pyrrole units are arranged so that their NH groups are oriented toward a central cavity. This arrangement allows for the formation of multiple, directional hydrogen bonds with anionic guests, such as halides, carboxylates, or phosphates. The cumulative effect of these hydrogen bonds leads to strong and selective anion binding. Additionally, the aromatic nature of pyrrole can contribute to the stabilization of the host-guest complex through π interactions and by providing a preorganized, rigid framework that enhances binding affinity and selectivity. The effectiveness of pyrrole-based hosts in anion recognition is a central concept in supramolecular chemistry, underpinning the design of sensors, transporters, and receptors for biologically and
